Burton looking for Raider revival after struggles of 2023 Antiques showcased at Virginia Highlands Festival HIGH SCHOOL NOTEBOOK: Wise Central's Emmah McAmis on Under Armour national title team; Some transfers and new coaching hires as well HIGH SCHOOL FOOTBALL: Smith aims high for Ridgeview LOCAL MOTORSPORTS: Chilhowie racer knows the Bristol Motor Speedway experience VHSL PRESEASON FOOTBALL PRIMER: Plenty of storylines as prep football begins in Virginia PREP FOOTBALL: Wise County Central's Owen Amos returns from injury BRIEFS: Tommy Buzzo steps down as E&H's defensive coordinator Trail town now boasts pathway lined with artwork\n\nOver the past decade, the python challenge has grabbed headlines for its incentive-based, only-in-Florida style of hunting as well as celebrity participation. This year, more than 600 people registered for the event, with two coming from Canada and 108 from other states.\n\nDuring the challenge, hunters will linger around designated areas spanning through western Broward County to the Tamiami Trail in the Big Cypress Wildlife Management Area, including other management areas like Southern Glades, Holey Land and Rotenberger.\n\nThe goal is to humanely kill as many pythons as possible, and prizes divide between three groups: professional hunters who work for the state, hunters who are active in the military or are veterans and novice hunters, which includes anyone who is not working as a state contracted python hunter.\n\nEach category has its own prizes, with $2,500 going to the person or team that kills the most pythons, $1,500 going to the runner-up for most kills and $1,000 going to whoever kills the longest python. The grand prize for the most kills in all categories gets a $10,000 prize.\n\nEach person can only win one prize, so if someone is tops in two categories, they will end up with the highest-valued prize and the next qualifying hunter gets the remaining prize.\n\nIn 2017, the South Florida Water Management District and the state began hiring contractors to handle its invasive python problem year round. According to the wildlife agency's website, through 2023, more than 11,000 pythons were removed by these contractors.\n\nLast year's challenge brought in 209 pythons and the grand prize winner was Paul Hobbs, who bagged 20 pythons. Also during 2023, Florida wildlife agency and district contractors removed about 2,200 pythons.\n\nAmy Siewe, the self-named Python Huntress, won a prize last year for catching a Burmese python measuring 10 feet and 9 inches. This year, she won't participate in the challenge due to a knee surgery but said she's not a fan of the annual challenge.\n\nSiewe, who used to work as a state contractor catching invasive pythons, said she believed the initial intent of the challenge was to bring awareness to the issue. Now, it's drawing large crowds of hunters, potentially scaring off pythons and killing native snakes they mistake as pythons, like corn snakes, brown water snakes or cottonmouths.\n\n\"Pythons don't take on their normal behavioral pattern because there's so much traffic and they'll come up and then they'll go back into the swamp,\" Siewe said.\n\nParticipants are required to undergo an online training, including information on how to identify Burmese pythons versus other snakes, Funck said. She said there's also an additional optional in-person training participants can attend to properly identify Burmese pythons.\n\n\"That's a huge part of what we do, is try to get the word out on how to identify these pythons, how to safely and humanely capture it,\" Funck said.\n\nCoastal Areas That Saw the Largest Increase in Water Temps Coastal Areas That Saw the Largest Increase in Water Temps {"uri": "8266901890", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-08-09", "time": "11:37:19", "dateTime": "2024-08-09T11:37:19Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-08-09T11:36:50Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.7960784435272217, "url": "https://www.nbcmiami.com/news/local/nvasive-enormous-insatiable-what-we-know-about-the-pythons-destroying-floridas-ecosystem/3386679/", "title": "Invasive, enormous, insatiable: What we know about the pythons destroying Florida's ecosystem", "body": "In South Florida, Burmese pythons are found primarily in and around the Everglades ecosystem, where the snake represents a major threat to native wildlife.\n\nIt's a danger to our ecosystem that Floridians know all too well.\n\nThe Burmese python is best known in South Florida for destroying the Everglades -- but what makes them such a threat?\n\nBecause these pythons are one of the state's most notorious invasive species, the Florida Fish and Wildlife Commission has implemented several efforts to remove the large nonvenomous constrictors from our ecosystem -- primarily by humanely killing them.\n\nTo incentivize their removal, Florida hosts annual python challenges. This year's event kicked off at midnight Friday, with hundreds registered to compete for $25,000 in cash prizes by wrangling snakes. Professional and novice hunters have until 5 p.m. on Aug. 18 to get to work.\n\nBut why do Burmese pythons need to be removed from the state, and what makes them such a danger to our environment?\n\nHere's everything you need to know about the slithery invaders:\n\nAn easy way to tell Burmese pythons apart from other snakes is by their size alone, according to McKayla Spencer, the FWC's Nonnative Fish and Wildlife Program Coordinator.\n\nAdult pythons caught in Florida average between six and nine feet long, but Spencer says it's not uncommon to find pythons much longer than that.\n\n\"The largest any of the native snakes in the United States get are below 10 feet,\" said Spencer. \"And we are regularly pulling pythons out of the Everglades that are over 10 feet long.\"\n\nJust as recently as July, a Florida hunter caught the longest Burmese python the state has ever seen.\n\nAt 19 feet and 125 pounds, the female snake is said to be the longest of its species to have ever been recorded, according to the Conservancy of Southwest Florida.\n\nAnother way to identify a Burmese python is by its unique markings.\n\nBurmese pythons are tan in color with dark blotches along the back and sides. The FWC describes the blotches as resembling puzzle pieces or the markings on a giraffe.\n\nThese pythons also have a pyramid or diamond-shaped head with a blunt nose.\n\nThe top of the head is marked by a dark, arrowhead-shaped wedge extending toward the nose with a tan-colored line through the middle.\n\nIn South Florida, Burmese pythons are found primarily in and around the Everglades ecosystem, where the snake represents a major threat to native wildlife.\n\nNatively, however, these pythons are found in India, lower China, the Malay Peninsula and some islands of the East Indies.\n\nBecause Burmese pythons are semi-aquatic, they are also often found near or in water.\n\n\"They're moving across that landscape with ease,\" said Spencer. \"Whereas in humans, we're terrestrial, so it makes it a lot more difficult for us to get out to these locations where a lot of these pythons are breeding.\"\n\nBurmese pythons are what scientists call \"generalist predators,\" meaning that they feed on a wide variety of species.\n\n\"That means they're not picky,\" says Spencer. \"They're eating our native mammals and birds and reptiles, which are obviously causing vast negative impacts in our Everglades ecosystem.\"\n\nBy preying upon native species, invasive Burmese pythons are creating a \"trophic cascade\" in the Everglades.\n\nA trophic cascade is an ecological phenomenon triggered by the addition (or removal) of a top predator -- in this case, the Burmese python.\n\nIt refers to the changes in the populations of predator and prey through the food chain, which often results in dramatic changes in the ecosystem.\n\nWhile pythons sometimes eat non-native species such as Norway rats, they also consume common native species, as well as threatened or endangered native species.\n\nFor instance, pythons have been known to feast on the federally threatened wood stork and the federally endangered Key Largo wood rats.\n\nThey have even been known to eat alligators, which are also federally protected by the Endangered Species Act.\n\n\"We imagine based on other systems where we've seen something similar, that when you essentially hit one level of a trophic cascade as hard as the Burmese pythons have -- especially in regards to mammals -- there's likely other cascading effects happening,\" said Spencer.\n\nResearch is currently underway to determine the impacts pythons have on native mammal species, according to the FWC.\n\nBurmese pythons can also pose a threat to human safety and may prey upon pets such as cats and dogs.\n\nBecause of their large size, adult Burmese pythons have few predators, with humans being an exception.\n\nSpencer says that Burmese pythons have a higher mortality rate when they are young and small. They come out of the egg at approximately two feet long.\n\nBecause this is still within the size class of many native species of snakes, Spencer says there are some animals that will prey upon them when they're that young.\n\nHowever, there is no evidence of a predator that is regularly preying upon a full-grown Burmese python, which Spencer says \"isn't much of a surprise\" considering how big they are.\n\nPythons arrived in Florida through the captive animal trade, and they were introduced through the accidental and/or purposeful release of these captive animals.\n\n\"1979 is when we had our first actual python data point,\" said Spencer. \"And in the 1990s is when we really started to see an uptick. That's when I think people started to realize there was a problem.\"\n\nAt this point, most experts consider pythons to be established in Florida from the southern tip of Lake Okeechobee to the northern part of the Keys.\n\n\"Some of that is because these animals are so hard to detect,\" said Spencer. \"By the time we're detecting them, there's a high likelihood that there's more than the single individual we're detecting.\"\n\nLike many of Florida's native snake species, Burmese pythons are semi-aquatic.\n\nHowever, Burmese pythons being semi-aquatic is a problem because it makes them harder for humans to remove.\n\nWith South Florida being a mostly semi-aquatic ecosystem, pythons can move across the landscape much easier than humans, because humans are terrestrial.\n\nThat limits our ability to get out to the locations where a lot of pythons are breeding.\n\nAdditionally, one of their main defenses is to camouflage. When these snakes try to hide themselves, they become very difficult to detect.\n\n\"You will be standing next to what you know is a 14, 15-foot python and you can't see it,\" said Spencer.\n\nThere is an ethical and legal obligation to ensure nonnative reptiles, such as Burmese pythons, are killed in a humane manner.\n\nNonnative reptiles are not protected in Florida except by the anti-cruelty law, which applies to all living animals in the state.\n\n\"They are terrible for the environment,\" said Spencer. \"We have got to be removing them. But, there are safe ways to capture them and there are humane ways to kill them.\"\n\n\"At this point, we're removing over 2,000 to 4,000 pythons a year,\" said Spencer. \"There's not a lot of options on what to do when you're removing that many animals.\"\n\nBecause Burmese pythons are wild snakes, they behave like wild animals would. This makes them difficult -- and dangerous -- to remove.\n\n\"When you go to capture a wild animal, they're going to defend themselves,\" Spencer said. \"They're going to strike, they're going to try to get away from you, they constrict, they musk.\"\n\nThe FWC works with partners to manage Burmese pythons in a variety of ways. Once these pythons are found, the primary method of removal is humane killing.\n\nThe American Veterinary Medical Association (AVMA) recommends a two-step process to prevent suffering and destroy the brain completely for humane killing of reptiles.\n\nThe first step should result in the animal losing consciousness immediately.\n\nThe second step, which immediately follows the first, should destroy the animal's brain by doing something called \"pithing,\" which prevents the animal from regaining consciousness.\n\n\"It's a very serious issue, and it's something that we want people taking seriously so that they understand the gravity of: you need to do this correctly and this is why,\" said Spencer.", "source": {"uri": "nbcmiami.com", "dataType": "news", "title": "NBC 6 South This year, more than 600 people registered for the event, with two coming from Canada and 108 from other states.\n\nDuring the challenge, hunters will linger around designated areas spanning through western Broward County to the Tamiami Trail in the Big Cypress Wildlife Management Area, including other management areas like Southern Glades, Holey Land and Rotenberger.\n\nThe goal is to humanely kill as many pythons as possible, and prizes divide between three groups: professional hunters who work for the state, hunters who are active in the military or are veterans and novice hunters, which includes anyone who is not working as a state contracted python hunter.\n\nEach category has its own prizes, with $2,500 going to the person or team that kills the most pythons, $1,500 going to the runner-up for most kills and $1,000 going to whoever kills the longest python. The grand prize for the most kills in all categories gets a $10,000 prize.\n\nEach person can only win one prize, so if someone is tops in two categories, they will end up with the highest-valued prize and the next qualifying hunter gets the remaining prize.\n\nIn 2017, the South Florida Water Management District and the state began hiring contractors to handle its invasive python problem year round. According to the wildlife agency's website, through 2023, more than 11,000 pythons were removed by these contractors.\n\nLast year's challenge brought in 209 pythons and the grand prize winner was Paul Hobbs, who bagged 20 pythons. Also during 2023, Florida wildlife agency and district contractors removed about 2,200 pythons.\n\nAmy Siewe, the self-named Python Huntress, won a prize last year for catching a Burmese python measuring 10 feet and 9 inches. This year, she won't participate in the challenge due to a knee surgery but said she's not a fan of the annual challenge.\n\nSiewe, who used to work as a state contractor catching invasive pythons, said she believed the initial intent of the challenge was to bring awareness to the issue. Now, it's drawing large crowds of hunters, potentially scaring off pythons and killing native snakes they mistake as pythons, like corn snakes, brown water snakes or cottonmouths.\n\n\"Pythons don't take on their normal behavioral pattern because there's so much traffic and they'll come up and then they'll go back into the swamp,\" Siewe said.\n\nParticipants are required to undergo an online training, including information on how to identify Burmese pythons versus other snakes, Funck said. She said there's also an additional optional in-person training participants can attend to properly identify Burmese pythons.\n\n\"That's a huge part of what we do, is try to get the word out on how to identify these pythons, how to safely and humanely capture it,\" Funck said.\n\nCoastal Areas That Saw the Largest Increase in Water Temps Coastal Areas That Saw the Largest Increase in Water Temps {"uri": "8266322888", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-08-09", "time": "04:34:25", "dateTime": "2024-08-09T04:34:25Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-08-09T04:33:54Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.7607843279838562, "url": "https://www.bostonglobe.com/2024/08/09/nation/snake-hunters-will-wrangle-invasive-burmese-pythons-everglades-during-floridas-10-day-challenge/", "title": "Snake hunters will wrangle invasive Burmese pythons in Everglades during Florida's 10-day challenge - The Boston Globe", "body": "Over the past decade, the python challenge has grabbed headlines for it's incentive-based, only-in-Florida style of hunting as well as celebrity participation. This year, more than 600 people registered for the event, with two coming from Canada and 108 from other states.\n\nDuring the challenge, hunters will linger around designated areas spanning through western Broward County to the Tamiami Trail in the Big Cypress Wildlife Management Area, including other management areas like Southern Glades, Holey Land and Rotenberger.\n\nThe goal is to humanely kill as many pythons as possible, and prizes divide between three groups: professional hunters who work for the state, hunters who are active in the military or are veterans and novice hunters, which includes anyone who is not working as a state contracted python hunter.\n\nEach category has its own prizes, with $2,500 going to the person or team that kills the most pythons, $1,500 going to the runner-up for most kills and $1,000 going to whoever kills the longest python. The grand prize for the most kills in all categories gets a $10,000 prize.\n\nEach person can only win one prize, so if someone is tops in two categories, they will end up with the highest-valued prize and the next qualifying hunter gets the remaining prize.\n\nIn 2017, the South Florida Water Management District and the state began hiring contractors to handle its invasive python problem year round. According to the wildlife agency's website, through 2023, more than 11,000 pythons have been removed by these contractors.\n\nLast year's challenge brought in 209 pythons and the grand prize winner was Paul Hobbs, who bagged 20 pythons. Also during 2023, Florida wildlife agency and district contractors removed about 2,200 pythons.\n\nAmy Siewe, the self-named Python Huntress, won a prize last year for catching a Burmese python measuring 10 feet and 9 inches (327 centimeters). This year, she won't be participating in the challenge due to a knee surgery but said she's not a fan of the annual challenge.\n\nSiewe, who used to work as a state contractor catching invasive pythons, said she believed the initial intent of the challenge was to bring awareness to the issue. Now, it's drawing large crowds of hunters, potentially scaring off pythons and potentially killing native snakes they mistake as pythons, like corn snakes, brown water snakes or cottonmouths.\n\n\"Pythons don't take on their normal behavioral pattern because there's so much traffic and they'll come up and then they'll go back into the swamp,\" Siewe said. \"I feel for myself, it's counterproductive.\"\n\nParticipants are required to undergo an online training, including information on how to identify Burmese pythons versus other snakes, Funck said. She has been working in journalism for seven years.\n\nThe annual Florida Python Challenge is here as the state continues its efforts to curb the population of invasive Burmese pythons.\n\nHundreds of snake hunters are currently registered for this year's 10-day hunt, which began Friday, Aug. 9, according to a news release from the Florida Fish and Wildlife Conservation Commission.\n\nEach contestant has a chance to win up to $10,000 for removing the most pythons throughout the multi-day competition, which ends on Sunday, Aug. 18.\n\nIn addition to catching the most pythons, participants at the novice, professional and military levels can also earn prizes for the longest python removed.\n\nIn a statement, \"Alligator Ron\" Bergeron, Governing Board Member of the South Florida Water Management District, said the challenge \"is a great way to get the public involved in understanding the unique Everglades ecosystem.\"\n\n\"Removing invasive pythons from across the Greater Everglades Ecosystem is critical, and we must do everything we can to combat this invasive species,\" Bergeron added.\n\nBurmese pythons \"are found primarily in and around the Everglades ecosystem in south Florida,\" according to the FWC.
